</div>
<p>The Constitution of Serbia defines it as a <a href="/wiki/Secular_state" title="Secular state">secular state</a> with guaranteed religious freedom. Orthodox Christians with 6,079,396 comprise 84.5% of country's population. The <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Orthodox_Church" title="Serbian Orthodox Church">Serbian Orthodox Church</a> is the largest and traditional church of the country, adherents of which are overwhelmingly Serbs. Other Orthodox Christian communities in Serbia include Montenegrins, Romanians, Vlachs, <a href="/wiki/Macedonians_(ethnic_group)" title="Macedonians (ethnic group)">Macedonians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulgarians" title="Bulgarians">Bulgarians</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Serbia" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism in Serbia">Roman Catholics</a> number 356,957 in Serbia, or roughly 6% of the population, mostly in Vojvodina (especially its northern part) which is home to minority ethnic groups such as Hungarians, Croats, <a href="/wiki/Bunjevci" title="Bunjevci">Bunjevci</a>, as well as to some Slovaks and Czechs.<sup id="cite_ref-popis2011.stat.rs_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popis2011.stat.rs-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> accounts for about 1% of the country's population, chiefly among <a href="/wiki/Slovaks_in_Vojvodina" class="mw-redirect" title="Slovaks in Vojvodina">Slovaks in Vojvodina</a> as well as among <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Reformed_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Hungarian Reformed Church">Reformed Hungarians</a>. <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Exarchate_of_Serbia_and_Montenegro" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostolic Exarchate of Serbia and Montenegro">Greek Catholic Church</a> is adhered by around 25,000 citizens (0.37% of the population), mostly <a href="/wiki/Pannonian_Rusyns" title="Pannonian Rusyns">Rusyns</a> in Vojvodina.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Muslims" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslims">Muslims</a>, with 222,282 or 3% of the population, form the third largest religious group. Islam has a strong historic following in the southern regions of Serbia, primarily in southern Raška. <a href="/wiki/Bosniaks" title="Bosniaks">Bosniaks</a> are the largest Islamic community in Serbia; estimates are that around a third of the country's Roma people are Muslim.</p>
<p>There are only 578 <a href="/wiki/Jews_in_Serbia" class="mw-redirect" title="Jews in Serbia">Jews</a> by faith in Serbia.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-161">[161]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Atheists" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheists">Atheists</a> numbered 80,053 or 1.1% of the population and an additional 4,070 declared themselves to be <a href="/wiki/Agnostic" class="mw-redirect" title="Agnostic">agnostics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Serbia" title="Languages of Serbia">Languages of Serbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian language</a></div>
<p>The official language is <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a>, native to 88% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-161">[161]</a></sup> Serbian is the only European language with active <a href="/wiki/Digraphia" title="Digraphia">digraphia</a>, using both <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyrillic">Cyrillic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaj%27s_Latin_alphabet" title="Gaj's Latin alphabet">Latin</a> alphabets. <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Cyrillic" class="mw-redirect" title="Serbian Cyrillic">Serbian Cyrillic</a> is designated in the Constitution as the "official script", compared to Latin's status of "script in official use".<sup id="cite_ref-Alexander2006_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander2006-162">[162]</a></sup> It was devised in 1814 by Serbian philologist <a href="/wiki/Vuk_Karad%C5%BEi%C4%87" title="Vuk Karadžić">Vuk Karadžić</a>, who created the alphabet on phonemic principles. A survey from 2014 showed that 47% of the Serbian population favours the Latin alphabet, 36% favours the Cyrillic one and 17% has no preference.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recognized minority languages are: <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_language" title="Bosnian language">Bosnian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovak_language" title="Slovak language">Slovak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">Croatian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rusyn_language" title="Rusyn language">Rusyn</a>. All these languages are in official use in municipalities or cities where the ethnic minority exceeds 15% of the total population.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> In Vojvodina, the provincial administration uses, besides Serbian, five other languages (Hungarian, Slovak, Croatian, Romanian and Rusyn).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Serbia" title="Economy of Serbia">Economy of Serbia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NIS-ova_zgrada_-_panoramio_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/NIS-ova_zgrada_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg/220px-NIS-ova_zgrada_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/NIS-ova_zgrada_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg/330px-NIS-ova_zgrada_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/NIS-ova_zgrada_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg/440px-NIS-ova_zgrada_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3440" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NIS-ova_zgrada_-_panoramio_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Naftna_Industrija_Srbije" title="Naftna Industrija Srbije">NIS</a> headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Novi_Sad" title="Novi Sad">Novi Sad</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Serbia has an emerging <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a> in upper-middle income range.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/IMF" class="mw-redirect" title="IMF">IMF</a>, Serbian <a href="/wiki/Nominal_GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominal GDP">nominal GDP</a> in 2015 is officially estimated at $36.56 billion or $5,102 per capita while <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> GDP was $97.27&#160;billion or $13,577 per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-imf2_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-imf2-3">[3]</a></sup> The economy is dominated by <a href="/wiki/Service_(economics)" title="Service (economics)">services</a> which accounts for 60.3% of GDP, followed by industry with 31.8% of GDP, and agriculture at 7.9% of GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_data_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_data-166">[166]</a></sup> The official currency of Serbia is <a href="/wiki/Serbian_dinar" title="Serbian dinar">Serbian dinar</a> (<a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">ISO</a> code: RSD), and the central bank is <a href="/wiki/National_Bank_of_Serbia" title="National Bank of Serbia">National Bank of Serbia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Belgrade_Stock_Exchange" title="Belgrade Stock Exchange">Belgrade Stock Exchange</a> is the only stock exchange in the country, with market capitalization of $8.65 billion (as of August 2014) and <a href="/wiki/BELEX15" title="BELEX15">BELEX15</a> as the main index representing the 15 most liquid stocks.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>The economy has been affected by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">global economic crisis</a>. After eight years of strong economic growth (average of 4.45% per year), Serbia entered the recession in 2009 with negative growth of −3% and again in 2012 with −1.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> As the government was fighting effects of crisis the public debt has more than doubled: from pre-crisis level of just under 30% to about 70% of GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>Active labor force at the end 2016 stood at 2.16 million, of whom 59.6% are employed in services sector, 23.9% are employed in the agriculture and 16.5% are employed in industry.<sup id="cite_ref-webrzs.stat.gov.rs_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webrzs.stat.gov.rs-171">[171]</a></sup> The average monthly net salary in June 2014 was 44,883 dinars (US$528,50).<sup id="cite_ref-salaries_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salaries-172">[172]</a></sup> The unemployment remains an acute problem, with rate of 13% as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Serbia&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-webrzs.stat.gov.rs_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webrzs.stat.gov.rs-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 2000, Serbia has attracted over $25 billion in <a href="/wiki/Foreign_direct_investment" title="Foreign direct investment">foreign direct investment</a> (FDI).<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Blue-chip corporations making investments in Serbia include: <a href="/wiki/FIAT" class="mw-redirect" title="FIAT">FIAT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bosch_GmbH" title="Robert Bosch GmbH">Bosch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Morris_International" title="Philip Morris International">Philip Morris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michelin" title="Michelin">Michelin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carlsberg_Group" title="Carlsberg Group">Carlsberg</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-belgrade.usembassy.gov_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-belgrade.usembassy.gov-174">[174]</a></sup> In the energy sector, Russian energy giants, <a href="/wiki/Gazprom" title="Gazprom">Gazprom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lukoil" title="Lukoil">Lukoil</a> have made large investments.<sup id="cite_ref-economy.gov.ru_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economy.gov.ru-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>Serbia has an unfavorable trade balance: imports exceed exports by 22%. Serbia's exports, however, recorded a steady growth in last couple of years reaching $14.8 billion in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> The country has <a href="/wiki/Free_trade_agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Free trade agreement">free trade agreements</a> with the <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">EFTA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_European_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="Central European Free Trade Agreement">CEFTA</a>, a preferential trade regime with the European Union, a <a href="/wiki/Generalized_System_of_Preferences" title="Generalized System of Preferences">Generalized System of Preferences</a> with the United States, and individual free trade agreements with Russia, Belarus, Kazakhstan, and Turkey.<sup id="cite_ref-SIEPA_-_Liberalized_Trade_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SIEPA_-_Liberalized_Trade-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Serbia" title="Agriculture in Serbia">Agriculture in Serbia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sauvignon_blanc_vlasotince_vineyards.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Sauvignon_blanc_vlasotince_vineyards.jpg/200px-Sauvignon_blanc_vlasotince_vineyards.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Sauvignon_blanc_vlasotince_vineyards.jpg/300px-Sauvignon_blanc_vlasotince_vineyards.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Sauvignon_blanc_vlasotince_vineyards.jpg/400px-Sauvignon_blanc_vlasotince_vineyards.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="2136" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sauvignon_blanc_vlasotince_vineyards.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In 2014 Serbia was 11th largest wine producer in Europe and 19th in the world.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Serbia has very favourable natural conditions (land and climate) for varied agricultural production. It has 5,056,000 ha of agricultural land (0.7 ha per capita), out of which 3,294,000 ha is arable land (0.45 ha per capita).<sup id="cite_ref-pks.rs_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pks.rs-178">[178]</a></sup> In 2016, Serbia exported agricultural and food products worth $3.2 billion, and the export-import ratio was 178%.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Agricultural exports constitute more than one-fifth of all Serbia's sales on the world market. Serbia is one of the largest provider of frozen fruit to the EU (largest to the French market, and 2nd largest to the German market).<sup id="cite_ref-siepa.gov.rs_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-siepa.gov.rs-180">[180]</a></sup> Agricultural production is most prominent in <a href="/wiki/Vojvodina" title="Vojvodina">Vojvodina</a> on the fertile Pannonian Plain. Other agricultural regions include <a href="/wiki/Ma%C4%8Dva" title="Mačva">Mačva</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pomoravlje_(region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pomoravlje (region)">Pomoravlje</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamnava" title="Tamnava">Tamnava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rasina_District" title="Rasina District">Rasina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jablanica_District" title="Jablanica District">Jablanica</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Agriculture_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agriculture-181">[181]</a></sup> In the structure of the agricultural production 70% is from the crop field production, and 30% is from the livestock production.<sup id="cite_ref-Agriculture_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agriculture-181">[181]</a></sup> Serbia is world's second largest producer of <a href="/wiki/Plum" title="Plum">plums</a> (582,485 tons; second to China), second largest of <a href="/wiki/Raspberry" title="Raspberry">raspberries</a> (89,602 tons, second to Poland), it is also significant producer of maize (6.48 million tons, ranked 32nd in the world) and wheat (2.07 million tons, ranked 35th in the world).<sup id="cite_ref-pod2.stat.gov.rs_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pod2.stat.gov.rs-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> Other important agricultural products are: <a href="/wiki/Sunflower" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunflower">sunflower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">sugar beet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybean</a>, potato, apple, pork meat, beef, <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a> and dairy.</p>
<p>There are 56,000 ha of <a href="/wiki/Serbian_wine" title="Serbian wine">vineyards in Serbia</a>, producing about 230 million litres of wine annually.<sup id="cite_ref-pod2.stat.gov.rs_100-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pod2.stat.gov.rs-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pks.rs_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pks.rs-178">[178]</a></sup> Most famous viticulture regions are located in Vojvodina and Šumadija.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_Serbia" title="Automotive industry in Serbia">Automotive industry in Serbia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2012-03-07_Motorshow_Geneva_4320.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/2012-03-07_Motorshow_Geneva_4320.JPG/220px-2012-03-07_Motorshow_Geneva_4320.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/2012-03-07_Motorshow_Geneva_4320.JPG/330px-2012-03-07_Motorshow_Geneva_4320.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/2012-03-07_Motorshow_Geneva_4320.JPG/440px-2012-03-07_Motorshow_Geneva_4320.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2012-03-07_Motorshow_Geneva_4320.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Fiat_500L" title="Fiat 500L">Fiat 500L</a>, assembled at the <a href="/wiki/Fiat_Chrysler_Automobiles" title="Fiat Chrysler Automobiles">FCA</a> plant in Kragujevac</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The industry is the economy sector which was hardest hit by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_757" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 757">UN sanctions and trade embargo</a> and <a href="/wiki/NATO_bombing_of_Yugoslavia" title="NATO bombing of Yugoslavia">NATO bombing</a> during the 1990s and transition to market economy during the 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> The industrial output saw dramatic downsizing: in 2013 it was expected to be only a half of that of 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Main industrial sectors include: automotive, mining, non-ferrous metals, food-processing, electronics, pharmaceuticals, clothes.</p>
<p>Automotive industry (with <a href="/wiki/Fiat_Chrysler_Automobiles" title="Fiat Chrysler Automobiles">Fiat Chrysler Automobiles</a> as a forebearer) is dominated by cluster located in <a href="/wiki/Kragujevac" title="Kragujevac">Kragujevac</a> and its vicinity, and contributes to export with about $2 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Serbia's mining industry is comparatively strong: Serbia is the 18th largest producer of coal (7th in the Europe) extracted from large deposits in <a href="/wiki/RB_Kolubara" title="RB Kolubara">Kolubara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kostolac_coal_mine" title="Kostolac coal mine">Kostolac</a> basins; it is also world's 23rd largest (3rd in Europe) producer of copper which is extracted by <a href="/wiki/RTB_Bor" title="RTB Bor">RTB Bor</a>, a large domestic copper mining company; significant gold extraction is developed around <a href="/wiki/Majdanpek" title="Majdanpek">Majdanpek</a>. Serbia notably manufactures <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">intel</a> smartphones named Tesla smartphones.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>Food industry is well known both regionally and internationally and is one of the strong points of the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> Some of the international brand-names established production in Serbia: <a href="/wiki/PepsiCo" title="PepsiCo">PepsiCo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nestl%C3%A9" title="Nestlé">Nestlé</a> in food-processing sector; <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> (Belgrade), <a href="/wiki/Heineken" title="Heineken">Heineken</a> (Novi Sad) and <a href="/wiki/Carlsberg_Group" title="Carlsberg Group">Carlsberg</a> (Bačka Palanka) in beverage industry; Nordzucker in sugar industry.<sup id="cite_ref-siepa.gov.rs_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-siepa.gov.rs-180">[180]</a></sup> Serbia's electronics industry had its peak in the 1980s and the industry today is only a third of what it was back then, but has witnessed a something of revival in last decade with investments of companies such as <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> (wind turbines) in Subotica, <a href="/wiki/Panasonic" title="Panasonic">Panasonic</a> (lighting devices) in Svilajnac, and <a href="/wiki/Gorenje" title="Gorenje">Gorenje</a> (electrical home appliances) in Valjevo.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> The pharmaceutical industry in Serbia comprises a dozen manufacturers of generic drugs, of which <a href="/wiki/Hemofarm_a.d." title="Hemofarm a.d.">Hemofarm</a> in Vršac and <a href="/wiki/Galenika_a.d." title="Galenika a.d.">Galenika</a> in Belgrade, account for 80% of production volume. Domestic production meets over 60% of the local demand.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Serbia" title="Energy in Serbia">Energy in Serbia</a></div>
<p>The energy sector is one of the largest and most important sectors to the country's economy. Serbia is a net exporter of electricity and importer of key fuels (such as oil and gas).</p>
<p>Serbia has an abundance of coal, and significant reserves of oil and gas. Serbia's proven reserves of 5.5 billion tons of <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">coal lignite</a> are the 5th largest in the world (second in Europe, after Germany).<sup id="cite_ref-b92.net_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b92.net-190">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Coal is found in two large deposits: <a href="/wiki/RB_Kolubara" title="RB Kolubara">Kolubara</a> (4 billion tons of reserves) and <a href="/wiki/Kostolac_coal_mine" title="Kostolac coal mine">Kostolac</a> (1.5 billion tons).<sup id="cite_ref-b92.net_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b92.net-190">[190]</a></sup> Despite being small on a world scale, Serbia's oil and gas resources (77.4 million tons of oil equivalent and 48.1 billion cubic meters, respectively) have a certain regional importance since they are largest in the region of former Yugoslavia as well as the Balkans (excluding Romania).<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> Almost 90% of the discovered oil and gas are to be found in <a href="/wiki/Banat" title="Banat">Banat</a> and those oil and gas fields are by size among the largest in the Pannonian basin but are average on a European scale.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dam_Serbia_Djerdap_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Dam_Serbia_Djerdap_2.jpg/220px-Dam_Serbia_Djerdap_2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Dam_Serbia_Djerdap_2.jpg/330px-Dam_Serbia_Djerdap_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Dam_Serbia_Djerdap_2.jpg/440px-Dam_Serbia_Djerdap_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dam_Serbia_Djerdap_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Iron_Gate_I_Hydroelectric_Power_Station" title="Iron Gate I Hydroelectric Power Station">Đerdap 1 Hydroelectric Power Station</a>, the largest dam on the Danube river and one of the largest hydro power stations in Europe</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The production of electricity in 2015 in Serbia was 36.5 billion kilowatt-hours (KWh), while the final electricity consumption amounted to 35.5 billion kilowatt-hours (KWh).<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Most of the electricity produced comes from thermal-power plants (72.7% of all electricity) and to a lesser degree from <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectric-power plants</a> (27.3%).<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> There are 6 lignite-operated <a href="/wiki/Fossil-fuel_power_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil-fuel power station">thermal-power plants</a> with an installed power of 3,936 MW; largest of which are 1,502 MW-<a href="/wiki/TPP_Nikola_Tesla" title="TPP Nikola Tesla">Nikola Tesla 1</a> and 1,160 MW-<a href="/wiki/TPP_Nikola_Tesla" title="TPP Nikola Tesla">Nikola Tesla 2</a>, both in Obrenovac.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> Total installed power of 9 hydroelectric-power plants is 2,831 MW, largest of which is <a href="/wiki/Iron_Gate_I_Hydroelectric_Power_Station" title="Iron Gate I Hydroelectric Power Station">Đerdap 1</a> with capacity of 1,026 MW.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> In addition to this, there are mazute and gas-operated thermal-power plants with an installed power of 353 MW.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> The entire production of electricity is concentrated in <a href="/wiki/Elektroprivreda_Srbije" title="Elektroprivreda Srbije">Elektroprivreda Srbije (EPS)</a>, public electric-utility power company.</p>
<p>The current oil production in Serbia amounts to over 1.1 million tons of oil equivalent<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> and satisfies some 43% of country's needs while the rest is imported.<sup id="cite_ref-uk.practicallaw.com_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uk.practicallaw.com-200">[200]</a></sup> National petrol company, <a href="/wiki/Naftna_Industrija_Srbije" title="Naftna Industrija Srbije">Naftna Industrija Srbije (NIS)</a>, was acquired in 2008 by <a href="/wiki/Gazprom_Neft" title="Gazprom Neft">Gazprom Neft</a>. The company has completed $700 million modernisation of oil-refinery in <a href="/wiki/Pan%C4%8Devo" title="Pančevo">Pančevo</a> (capacity of 4.8 million tons) and is currently in the midst of converting oil refinery in <a href="/wiki/Novi_Sad" title="Novi Sad">Novi Sad</a> into lubricants-only refinery. It also operates network of 334 filling stations in Serbia (74% of domestic market) and additional 36 stations in <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>, 31 in <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, and 28 in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> There are 155 kilometers of crude oil pipelines connecting Pančevo and Novi Sad refineries as a part of trans-national <a href="/wiki/Adria_oil_pipeline" title="Adria oil pipeline">Adria oil pipeline</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p>Serbia is heavily dependent on foreign sources of natural gas, with only 17% coming from domestic production (totalling 491 million cubic meters in 2012) and the rest is imported, mainly from Russia (via gas pipelines that run through Ukraine and Hungary).<sup id="cite_ref-uk.practicallaw.com_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uk.practicallaw.com-200">[200]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Srbijagas" title="Srbijagas">Srbijagas</a>, public gas company, operates the natural gas transportation system which comprise 3,177 kilometers of trunk and regional natural gas pipelines and a 450 million cubic meter underground gas storage facility at <a href="/wiki/Banatski_Dvor" title="Banatski Dvor">Banatski Dvor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Serbia" title="Transport in Serbia">Transport in Serbia</a></div>
<table class="toccolours" style="float:left; margin:0 0 1em 1em; border:1px #ddd solid;">
<tr>
<td></td>
</tr>
<tr style="text-align:center;">
<td style="width:130px;"><a href="/wiki/File:J26_933_Bf_Ba%C4%8Dka_Topola,_413_029.jpg" class="image"><img alt="J26 933 Bf Bačka Topola, 413 029.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/J26_933_Bf_Ba%C4%8Dka_Topola%2C_413_029.jpg/210px-J26_933_Bf_Ba%C4%8Dka_Topola%2C_413_029.jpg" width="210" height="140" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/J26_933_Bf_Ba%C4%8Dka_Topola%2C_413_029.jpg/315px-J26_933_Bf_Ba%C4%8Dka_Topola%2C_413_029.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/J26_933_Bf_Ba%C4%8Dka_Topola%2C_413_029.jpg/420px-J26_933_Bf_Ba%C4%8Dka_Topola%2C_413_029.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5604" data-file-height="3736" /></a><br />
<small><a href="/wiki/Serbian_Railways" title="Serbian Railways">Serbian Railways</a> trainset<br /></small></td>
<td style="width:130px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Air_Serbia_ATR-72-202_taking_off_from_Belgrade_Airport.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Air Serbia ATR-72-202 taking off from Belgrade Airport.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Air_Serbia_ATR-72-202_taking_off_from_Belgrade_Airport.jpg/210px-Air_Serbia_ATR-72-202_taking_off_from_Belgrade_Airport.jpg" width="210" height="140" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Air_Serbia_ATR-72-202_taking_off_from_Belgrade_Airport.jpg/316px-Air_Serbia_ATR-72-202_taking_off_from_Belgrade_Airport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Air_Serbia_ATR-72-202_taking_off_from_Belgrade_Airport.jpg/421px-Air_Serbia_ATR-72-202_taking_off_from_Belgrade_Airport.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="682" /></a><br />
<small><a href="/wiki/Air_Serbia" title="Air Serbia">Air Serbia</a> planes<br /></small></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Serbia has a strategic transportation location since the country's backbone, <a href="/wiki/Great_Morava" title="Great Morava">Morava Valley</a>, represents by far the easiest route of land travel from continental Europe to <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>.</p>
<p>Serbian road network carries the bulk of traffic in the country. Total length of roads is 45,419&#160;km of which 782&#160;km are "class-Ia state roads" (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Motorways" class="mw-redirect" title="Motorways">motorways</a>); 4,481&#160;km are "class-Ib state roads" (national roads); 10,941&#160;km are "class-II state roads" (regional roads) and 23,780&#160;km are "municipal roads".<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> The road network, except for the most of class-Ia roads, are of comparatively lower quality to the Western European standards because of lack of financial resources for their maintenance in the last 20 years.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:187px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New_map_of_motorways_in_Serbia.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/New_map_of_motorways_in_Serbia.svg/185px-New_map_of_motorways_in_Serbia.svg.png" width="185" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/New_map_of_motorways_in_Serbia.svg/278px-New_map_of_motorways_in_Serbia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/New_map_of_motorways_in_Serbia.svg/370px-New_map_of_motorways_in_Serbia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="703" data-file-height="1042" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_map_of_motorways_in_Serbia.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Motorway network
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:green; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;finished</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:red; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;under construction</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:gray; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;planned</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are currently 124 kilometers of motorways under construction: two sections 34&#160;km-long of the <a href="/wiki/A1_motorway_(Serbia)" title="A1 motorway (Serbia)">A1 motorway</a> (from south of <a href="/wiki/Leskovac" title="Leskovac">Leskovac</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bujanovac" title="Bujanovac">Bujanovac</a>), 67&#160;km-long segment of <a href="/wiki/A2_motorway_(Serbia)" title="A2 motorway (Serbia)">A2</a> (between <a href="/wiki/Belgrade" title="Belgrade">Belgrade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ljig" title="Ljig">Ljig</a>), and 23 kilometers on the <a href="/wiki/A4_motorway_(Serbia)" title="A4 motorway (Serbia)">A4</a> (east of <a href="/wiki/Ni%C5%A1" title="Niš">Niš</a> to the Bulgarian border).<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coach_(bus)" title="Coach (bus)">Coach</a> transport is very extensive: almost every place in the country is connected by bus, from largest cities to the villages; in addition there are international routes (mainly to countries of Western Europe with large Serb diaspora). Routes, both domestic and international, are served by more than 100 bus companies, biggest of which are <a href="/wiki/Lasta_Beograd" title="Lasta Beograd">Lasta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ni%C5%A1-Ekspres" title="Niš-Ekspres">Niš-Ekspres</a>. As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Serbia&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there were 1,833,215 registered passenger cars or 1 passenger car per 3.8 inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>Serbia has 3,819 kilometers of rail tracks, of which 1,279 are electrified and 283 kilometers are double-track railroad.<sup id="cite_ref-pod2.stat.gov.rs_100-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pod2.stat.gov.rs-100">[100]</a></sup> The major rail hub is Belgrade (and to a lesser degree Niš), while the most important railroads include: <a href="/wiki/Belgrade%E2%80%93Bar_railway" title="Belgrade–Bar railway">Belgrade–Bar (Montenegro)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgrade-%C5%A0id_railway" class="mw-redirect" title="Belgrade-Šid railway">Belgrade–Šid–Zagreb (Croatia)/Belgrade–Niš–Sofia (Bulgaria)</a> (part of <a href="/wiki/Pan-European_Corridor_X" title="Pan-European Corridor X">Pan-European Corridor X</a>), Belgrade–Subotica–Budapest (Hungary) and Niš–Thessaloniki (Greece). Although still a major mode of freight transportation, railroads face increasing problems with the maintenance of the infrastructure and lowering speeds. All rail services are operated by public rail company, <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Railways" title="Serbian Railways">Serbian Railways</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> There are only two airports with regular passenger traffic: <a href="/wiki/Belgrade_Nikola_Tesla_Airport" title="Belgrade Nikola Tesla Airport">Belgrade Nikola Tesla Airport</a> served almost 5 million passengers in 2016, and is a hub of flagship carrier <a href="/wiki/Air_Serbia" title="Air Serbia">Air Serbia</a> which carried some 2.6 million passengers in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ni%C5%A1_Constantine_the_Great_Airport" title="Niš Constantine the Great Airport">Niš Constantine the Great Airport</a> is mainly catering <a href="/wiki/Low-cost_airline" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-cost airline">low-cost airlines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup></p>
<p>Serbia has a developed inland water transport since there are 1,716 kilometers of navigable inland waterways (1,043&#160;km of navigable rivers and 673&#160;km of navigable canals), which are almost all located in northern third of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-pod2.stat.gov.rs_100-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pod2.stat.gov.rs-100">[100]</a></sup> The most important inland waterway is the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> (part of Pan-European Corridor VII). Other navigable rivers include <a href="/wiki/Sava" title="Sava">Sava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tisza" title="Tisza">Tisza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bega_River_(Tisza)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bega River (Tisza)">Begej</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timi%C5%9F_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Timiş River">Timiş River</a>, all of which connect Serbia with Northern and Western Europe through the <a href="/wiki/Rhine%E2%80%93Main%E2%80%93Danube_Canal" title="Rhine–Main–Danube Canal">Rhine–Main–Danube Canal</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> route, to Eastern Europe via the Tisza, Begej and Danube Black Sea routes, and to Southern Europe via the Sava river. More than 2 million tons of cargo were transported on Serbian rivers and canals in 2016 while the largest river ports are: Novi Sad, Belgrade, Pančevo, <a href="/wiki/Smederevo" title="Smederevo">Smederevo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prahovo" title="Prahovo">Prahovo</a> and Šabac.<sup id="cite_ref-pod2.stat.gov.rs_100-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pod2.stat.gov.rs-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Telecommunications">Telecommunications</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Serbia" title="Telecommunications in Serbia">Telecommunications in Serbia</a></div>
<p>Fixed telephone lines connect 89% of households in Serbia, and with about 9.1 million users the number of cellphones surpasses the total population of Serbia by 29%. The largest mobile operator is <a href="/wiki/Telekom_Srbija" title="Telekom Srbija">Telekom Srbija</a> with 4.2 million subscribers, followed by <a href="/wiki/Telenor_Serbia" title="Telenor Serbia">Telenor</a> with 2.9 million users and <a href="/wiki/Vip_mobile" title="Vip mobile">Vip mobile</a> with about 2 million.<sup id="cite_ref-ratel.rs_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ratel.rs-217">[217]</a></sup> Some 64.7% of households have Internet connection,<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> while practically the same percentage (64.2%) are provided with <a href="/wiki/Pay_television" title="Pay television">pay television services</a> (i.e. 37% cable television, 16% IPTV, and 11% satellite).<sup id="cite_ref-ratel.rs_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ratel.rs-217">[217]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Digital_television_transition" title="Digital television transition">Digital television transition</a> has been completed in 2015 with <a href="/wiki/DVB-T2" title="DVB-T2">DVB-T2</a> standard for signal transmission.<sup id="cite_ref-EJC_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EJC-219">[219]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Serbia" title="Tourism in Serbia">Tourism in Serbia</a></div>
<p>Serbia is not a mass-tourism destination but nevertheless has a diverse range of touristic products.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> In 2016, total of over 2.7 million tourists were recorded in accommodations, of which some 1.28 million were foreign.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> Foreign exchange earnings from tourism were estimated at $1.15 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tourism is mainly focused on the mountains and spas of the country, which are mostly visited by domestic tourists, as well as <a href="/wiki/Belgrade" title="Belgrade">Belgrade</a> which is preferred choice of foreign tourists.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> The most famous mountain resorts are <a href="/wiki/Kopaonik" title="Kopaonik">Kopaonik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Mountains" title="Balkan Mountains">Stara Planina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zlatibor" title="Zlatibor">Zlatibor</a>. There are also many <a href="/wiki/Spa" title="Spa">spas</a> in Serbia, the biggest of which is <a href="/wiki/Vrnja%C4%8Dka_Banja" title="Vrnjačka Banja">Vrnjačka Banja</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soko_Banja" class="mw-redirect" title="Soko Banja">Soko Banja</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Banja_Kovilja%C4%8Da" title="Banja Koviljača">Banja Koviljača</a>. City-break and conference tourism is developed in Belgrade (which was visited by 517,401 foreign tourists in 2013, more than a half of all international visits to the country) and to a lesser degree <a href="/wiki/Novi_Sad" title="Novi Sad">Novi Sad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> Other touristic products that Serbia offer are natural wonders like <a href="/wiki/Djavolja_varos" class="mw-redirect" title="Djavolja varos">Đavolja varoš</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> Christian pilgrimage to the many <a href="/wiki/List_of_Serbian_Orthodox_monasteries" title="List of Serbian Orthodox monasteries">Orthodox monasteries</a> across the country<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> and the river cruising along the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>. There are several internationally popular music festivals held in Serbia, such as <a href="/wiki/Exit_(festival)" title="Exit (festival)">EXIT</a> (with 25–30,000 foreign visitors coming from 60 different countries) and the <a href="/wiki/Gu%C4%8Da_trumpet_festival" class="mw-redirect" title="Guča trumpet festival">Guča trumpet festival</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tnone center">
